Jun 13, 2024 PEI -4 Purpose Critical analysis of PEI education Department's naming, definitions, purpose, role, and organizational structure.… ▸ more
Critical analysis of PEI education Department's naming, definitions, purpose, role, and organizational structure. Criticizes department name "Education and Early Years" (formerly "Lifelong Learning") as unnecessarily limiting when education encompasses all ages. Notes Department lacks formal education definition; School Act revision replaced "education" definition with "school" definition. Questions whether education should serve all Islanders versus only children. Reviews official purposes from multiple sources (School Administrative Handbook, Department Website, Philosophy documents) and notes inconsistencies and insufficient specificity for program planning/progress measurement. Lists comprehensive departmental goals (2015-16) spanning citizenship, critical thinking, knowledge acquisition, creativity, workplace skills, mental/physical health, bilingualism, gender equality, human rights. Dated June 13, 2024. Argues for clarity, transparency, and comprehensive documentation.

Frames investigation as foundational for reform work. (Dated May 22, 2024)

Jun 5, 2023 Issues-7 Education (175 words):** Don Glendenning presents a detailed analysis of structural issues requiring public dialogue about… ▸ more
(175 words):** Don Glendenning presents a detailed analysis of structural issues requiring public dialogue about education's future in PEI. He emphasizes that education is fundamentally complex due to learner diversity, and systems require clear purposes, functions, and structures. Key issues: (1) Terminology—educators use single terms for multiple concepts without definitions, conflating education with schooling; (2) Purpose—PEI's purpose statements lack detail needed for development and assessment; (3) Structure—failing to organize around identified functions; (4) Autonomy—OECD research shows schools with operational autonomy achieve better PISA results; (5) Accountability—requires defining missions, values, goals, and ensuring transparent communication; (6) Standards and grading—conflicts arise when teachers both teach and grade; competency reporting preferred; (7) Human and social capital—isolated teachers lack collaborative culture. Don advocates moving from product-focused (credentials) to service-focused education, and calls for public engagement in systemic change, extending an invitation to Islanders (June 5, 2023).

Extensive analysis of terminology confusion, purpose necessity, structural function-alignment, autonomy advantages (OECD PISA 13-country study findings), accountability systems (compliance, professional norms, results), standards application tensions (teaching vs. grading role conflicts), benchmarks (Grade 12 democratic society competency example), and collaborative engagement requirements. Proposes establishing independent qualification bodies separate from teacher assessment roles. Emphasizes education system modernization necessity. (Dated May 23, 2023)

2023 PEI 3 Purpose Comparative analysis of education purpose statements across jurisdictions and philosophical frameworks. Don examines… ▸ more
Comparative analysis of education purpose statements across jurisdictions and philosophical frameworks. Don examines purposes from: Fielding (personal relationships priority), Alberta (economic competitiveness skills), New Brunswick (lifelong learning attributes), various anonymous sources emphasizing postsecondary entrance, life navigation, democratic citizenship, engaged participation, Home and School (general education level), OECD (multi-faceted world citizenship), Ontario (skilled caring citizens), Quebec (instruction, socialization, qualifications). PEI documents surveyed: School Administrative Handbook (children's meaningful societal place), Department Website (quality programs/curriculum delivery), philosophy documents (societal place preparation). Concerns noted: limitations to children only, insufficient specificity for planning/measurement. Advocates for purpose clarity enabling program design and progress assessment. Dated Nov 7, 2023. Demonstrates Don's comparative policy analysis approach across Canadian/international contexts.

Don discusses the Oregon Department of Education's "life roles" model (individual, family member, citizen, student, producer, consumer) and applies it to education's purpose. He criticizes the lack of clear purpose in government education investment, advocating that governments explicitly define what they hope to accomplish through education to enable system effectiveness. This reflective piece dates from around 2023.

Dec 21, 2020 100-Final The December 21, 2020 special issue 100 of Open Education serves as both a milestone and farewell. Don shares a… ▸ more
The December 21, 2020 special issue 100 of Open Education serves as both a milestone and farewell. Don shares a profound personal memory: departing home on January 2, 1947 at age 17 for Teachers' College in Fredericton with a plaque reading "I shall pass this way but once / Any good thing therefore that I can do or kindness that I may show / Let me do it now / Let me not defer it nor neglect it / For I shall not pass this way again." Don notes this inscription guided his decision-making throughout his life. The final issue sends season's greetings to readers and friends worldwide, marking the conclusion of a newsletter that began in 2013 and evolved through 100 editions. This poignant closing document captures Don's lifelong commitment to educational excellence and his belief that each moment offers opportunity for meaningful contribution. It reveals the spiritual and philosophical foundation underlying his professional career.

December 2020 14 Overhaul December 2020 paper analyzing structural impediments to educational improvement, drawing on World Bank research and… ▸ more
December 2020 paper analyzing structural impediments to educational improvement, drawing on World Bank research and Don's experience chairing community education discussions. He identifies political context challenges (politicization within government departments affecting long-term planning), mission ambiguity, organizational structure limitations, insufficient autonomy, unclear accountability, fragmented culture treating education as product rather than service, and inadequate instructional support materials. Don argues that many functions should delegate to schools and intermediary bodies, citing OECD evidence that student performance improves with school autonomy. He advocates treating education as a service to learners rather than a product to deliver, emphasizing learner-centered rather than teacher-centered support materials. The paper calls for clear vision-setting as prerequisite for structural reform.

Jun 4, 2020 89 Accoubtability Article No. 89 (June 4, 2020) titled "PEI Ignores Recommendations to Improve Education" restates PEI's official… ▸ more
Article No. 89 (June 4, 2020) titled "PEI Ignores Recommendations to Improve Education" restates PEI's official education goals (from policy documents) and provides formal definitions of accountability from the Canadian Oxford Dictionary. Don lists 15 official education goals (ranging from developing intellectual curiosity and critical thinking to understanding gender equity and human rights) and notes that although family and community roles are important, the school curriculum is considered prime in addressing these goals. He emphasizes that accountability is essential to any sustained human relationship and requires both parties (educator and learner) to account for their actions.

May 2020 D 20. Restructuring May 2020 paper on reorganizing education services structure. Argues current system reflects "schooling not education."… ▸ more
May 2020 paper on reorganizing education services structure. Argues current system reflects "schooling not education." Proposes function-based management with six key service components: (1) Governance & Leadership (planning, liaison, advocacy, quality control); (2) Instruction (teaching, demonstration, coaching, diagnosis); (3) Information & Counselling (assessment, referral, advocacy); (4) Quality Assurance (credentials, qualifications); (5) Funding (capital, operating); (6) Learning Products (texts, technologies, MOOCs). Represents mature articulation of systems thinking about educational delivery.

Article No. 89 (April 27, 2020) on "Education for Employment" discusses how schools traditionally assessed employment-preparedness through equipment quality, time allocation, and teacher qualifications, and notes the shift toward dynamic rating scales. Don briefly acknowledges that "vocational" terminology is disappearing while the purpose remains unclear. He describes community-based performance rating criteria (accuracy, consistency, assistance needed, time to complete, fluency, complexity, confidence) and notes that dynamic rating scales include significant judgment components that some student record systems cannot accommodate. The piece is incomplete and fragmentary.

Apr 15, 2020 88 Learning This undated piece (April 15, 2020, No. 88) presents Don's foundational beliefs about learners and education, again… ▸ more
This undated piece (April 15, 2020, No. 88) presents Don's foundational beliefs about learners and education, again referencing Dewey's emphasis on understanding people's beliefs. Key beliefs include: learning is personal, most people can learn needed modern-economy skills, teachers design experiences for behavioral change, grading is not an educational function, and qualifications differ from credentials. The document also lists government investment rationales and responsibilities, emphasizing that learners need advocates, schools lack self-correcting mechanisms, and instruction should prepare students for qualifications rather than just credentials.

Feb 20, 2020 90-- Foundational Bellief Article No. 90 (February 20, 2020) outlines foundational beliefs (learning is behavioral change, observable and… ▸ more
Article No. 90 (February 20, 2020) outlines foundational beliefs (learning is behavioral change, observable and measurable; education is gestalt of formative experiences; teaching creates behavioral-change strategies; groups don't learn but individuals learn in groups; most people can learn modern-economy skills). The piece references OECD research on teacher quality, noting that "the largest source of variation in student learning is attributable to differences in what students bring to school" and that teacher factors are more important than school variables influencing achievement. Don emphasizes that teacher quality matters more than academic knowledge alone—conveying ideas effectively, creating learning environments, enthusiasm, and creativity are equally important. This piece synthesizes research with his educational philosophy.

2020 75 School functions Don articulates his vision of teacher roles for innovative schools in 2020. Rather than including grading and… ▸ more
Don articulates his vision of teacher roles for innovative schools in 2020. Rather than including grading and evaluation, he identifies twelve core instructor functions: assisting in program design, informing and counselling, creating learning environments, evaluating resources, supporting self-learning, communication, community interaction, learning management, resource development, competency building, administrative duties, and needs diagnosis. Notably absent from this list is grading, which Don designates as a regulatory rather than teaching function. He references international practice where qualification bodies handle assessment, noting Canada and USA as exceptions. Includes brief discussion of federal government's lapsed leadership in vocational training and historical technical education programs (Technical Education Act of 1919).

2020 Curriculum-2 `Notes Synthesizes curriculum development research and Don's personal analysis of PEI education needs. Reviews provincial… ▸ more
Synthesizes curriculum development research and Don's personal analysis of PEI education needs. Reviews provincial education literature (Alberta, Ontario, BC, Canada 2020) and emerging competency-based approaches. Discusses William Spady's philosophy that practitioners, not educators alone, should identify outcomes. Notes that curriculum-based outcomes appear unique to Canada. Reports conversations with Island employers, parents, students, and educators revealing gaps in understanding of curriculum terms (outcomes, competencies, skills). Addresses mathematics education as key competency for new economy while remaining underperforming goal. Provides detailed observations and recommendations for PEI system improvements, including adoption of competency basis, dynamic rating scales, and independent assessment body.

Sep 15, 2019 80 Turmoil in Curriculum Open Education No. 80 (September 15, 2019), authored by Don Glendenning discussing curriculum reform movements in… ▸ more
Open Education No. 80 (September 15, 2019), authored by Don Glendenning discussing curriculum reform movements in Canada and internationally. He notes Ontario's introduction of math teacher testing, Alberta's curriculum overhaul emphasizing essential skills, UK and Australia adding numeracy courses. Don identifies key questions: content expansion vs. fluency improvement; competency versus content base; community input on practical math needs; student practice application; support material format; teacher math application training; citizen numeracy requirements. He proposes overarching curriculum principles: separate teaching and grading; replace credentials with qualifications; base programs on needed/practiced skills; implement dynamic assessment; modernize report cards; improve accountability; introduce differentiated staffing; ensure professional autonomy.

Feb 20, 2019 Numeracy ask A persuasive fundraising letter dated February 20, 2019, from Don Glendenning to Robert Irving, President of Cavendish… ▸ more
A persuasive fundraising letter dated February 20, 2019, from Don Glendenning to Robert Irving, President of Cavendish Farms, requesting financial support for the Numeracy Project. Don articulates the problem: while PEI math test scores have risen nationally, practical numeracy skills on the shop floor remain inadequate. The letter demonstrates Don's strategic thinking about applied mathematics education and his networking within the business community.

2019 Funding-1 Policy analysis document examining funding and systemic coordination of mathematics education in PEI. Establishes… ▸ more
Policy analysis document examining funding and systemic coordination of mathematics education in PEI. Establishes agreement on skill difference attribution through four factors: number of skills learned, nature of skills, student performance levels, and clustering patterns. Documents misalignment between public school outcomes-based curriculum and postsecondary competency-based approaches, arguing for unified framework. Identifies seven systemic conclusions: fragmented instruction delivery, skill-level-clustering differences, outcome-competency misalignment, instruction-grading conflicts, regulatory vs. instructional functions, teacher authorization inconsistencies, and lack of agreed-upon definitions. Addresses educational philosophy gaps (30-year-old, public school only), absent coordination mechanisms, inconsistent assessment criteria, and content description variations. Notes 2019 mathematics curriculum review timeline. Recommends standard definitions, common competency list, and independent achievement level determination body.

Occasional paper from 2018 examining graduation's definition and purpose. Traces concept etymology (from Latin "gradus" meaning step), exploring multiple interpretations: program completion, years-of-study completion, meeting institution requirements, pay classification determinant. Questions what society expects of high school graduates. Demonstrates Don's careful analytical approach to taken-for-granted educational concepts and his commitment to rigorous definitional clarity in educational discourse.

Oct 15, 2017 DACUM-50 Don Substantial historical essay dated October 15, 2017, documenting DACUM (Developing a Curriculum), a Canadian… ▸ more
Substantial historical essay dated October 15, 2017, documenting DACUM (Developing a Curriculum), a Canadian educational achievement. Chronicles post-WWII industrial training needs, Canada's Technical and Vocational Training Assistance Act (1960), and the Nova Scotia NewStart Corporation (1967) where Robert Adams developed competency-based curriculum analysis. Details Holland College's 1969 establishment and adoption of Adams' DACUM methodology, creating STEP (Self Training and Evaluation Process). Describes how Ohio State's Robert Norton helped spread DACUM throughout USA. Provides Adams' biography (1932-2016). Scholarly yet accessible, this document reflects Don's deep engagement with educational innovation history and his institution's foundational practices.

Feb 12, 2017 1-3 Discussion points Talking points document from February 12, 2017 outlining key concepts for discussing proficiency levels in education.… ▸ more
Talking points document from February 12, 2017 outlining key concepts for discussing proficiency levels in education. It defines proficiency as determined by two elements: what one can do and how well one can do it. The document identifies employers' inability to rely on high school grades as measures of competency and references Wolff's assessment framework. It contrasts assessment (learner-centered, interactive, formative) with evaluation (grades-focused). Includes Glendenning's core beliefs: diplomas are inadequate for proficiency indication, grades confuse age and proficiency, grading is not a school function, employment mobility requires recognized qualifications, and most developed countries have external qualification authorities. The document concludes with a proposal to develop an independent assessment service for math and communication skills proficiency.

Dec 17, 2016 D-4 Gap Open Education No. 68 (December 17, 2016), authored by Don Glendenning discussing responsibility allocation in… ▸ more
Open Education No. 68 (December 17, 2016), authored by Don Glendenning discussing responsibility allocation in education between learners, parents, teachers, and government. He argues learners are responsible for their own learning (with parental support), but government's prescriptive education model shifts responsibility from family to bureaucracy. Don critiques that education leadership has imposed their model on citizens while assuming responsibility for defining what should be learned and when. He notes the gap: unlike health or justice, limited independent guidance exists for parents and learners about next steps, resulting in young people entering work or post-secondary education unprepared. Don questions what education services Islanders need and what delivery methods would serve them best, challenging fundamental assumptions about education's organization and purpose.

2016 02 Personal framework Extensive 2016 document presenting Don's comprehensive personal educational philosophy evolved over years of… ▸ more
Extensive 2016 document presenting Don's comprehensive personal educational philosophy evolved over years of professional experience. Addresses education as most important societal issue and spans multiple sections: quality education characteristics (accessible, affordable, flexible, relevant, accountable, transparent), systemic gaps (lacks philosophy, suffers from politicization/bureaucracy), barriers to change, organizational problems (top-down, formula-driven, out of touch with clients), and proposed solutions. Includes Don's core beliefs about learner-centered delivery, distinguishing education from schooling, mathematics as prerequisite for opportunity, and need for external qualification authorities. Proposes priority changes from simple (exemptions/challenge for credit) to structural (depoliticizing education, establishing independent assessment bodies). Major theme: education system is production-oriented rather than development-oriented and requires fundamental restructuring.

2016 2016 CVA Strategy Summit These notes from a 2016 CVA Strategy Summit reflect Don's perspective on the association's history, current status, and… ▸ more
These notes from a 2016 CVA Strategy Summit reflect Don's perspective on the association's history, current status, and future directions. Don notes the CVA's unique individual (not institutional) membership model, broad cross-sector participation, historical government partnership roles, and the 1970 emergence of ACCC which drew administrators toward institutional funding. He observes vocational education's shift from content-based to performance-measured definitions and the federal government's reduced leverage for change. Don argues the CVA could become a catalyst for educational change, particularly around "transition" from school to employment. Responding to summit questions about high points, current functioning, and future vision, Don emphasizes the value of personal networking and provincial connections developed at annual conferences. He proposes federal-provincial initiatives using "transition" as an organizing concept and questions whether the CVA could help coordinate training initiatives across regions.

Oct 20, 2015 A-5 Newsletter 33 Achievement Levels Newsletter article as Guest Editor for Open Education (Pédagogie ouverte) published October 20, 2015. Don critiques… ▸ more
Newsletter article as Guest Editor for Open Education (Pédagogie ouverte) published October 20, 2015. Don critiques Australian and PEI classroom achievement disparities reported by the Grattan Institute, arguing that schools conflate grade level with achievement level. He challenges the assumption that wide achievement ranges are inevitable, proposing instead that schools choose whether to make time or standards the constant in education.

Jul 15, 2015 8 Math levels A refined proposal document (dated July 15, 2015) further developing the mathematics proficiency assessment system.… ▸ more
A refined proposal document (dated July 15, 2015) further developing the mathematics proficiency assessment system. This version distills key findings: current education lacks recognized subject analysis and separates teaching from assessment functions. Don proposes a systematic approach using mathematics as a pilot: analyze the field, identify proficiency levels, produce assessment models, and establish independent assessment bodies. Services would be independent of schools, employers, and government; non-competitive; and confidential. The document cites successful existing models (Apprenticeship Red Seal, Red Cross Swim Program, Royal Canadian Conservatory of Music) demonstrating that subject-specific proficiency certification is viable and beneficial.

May 11, 2014 CHOICE AS AN AGENT OF CHANGE Major essay (May 11, 2014) advocating choice as educational reform mechanism. Don argues individuals differ in… ▸ more
Major essay (May 11, 2014) advocating choice as educational reform mechanism. Don argues individuals differ in experiences, interests, learning styles; choice-making is an important educational skill; individuals should take responsibility for choices; consumer choices drive service improvement. Contrasts limited educational choice with choice available in other sectors (church, professional services). References Bay County (Florida) School Authority 2005 open enrollment example. Proposes four choice dimensions: School choice (location), teacher choice (chemistry between teacher and student), scheduling choice (flexibility in time spent in school), and graduation date choice (based on competency not age). Argues choice removes excuses, increases accountability, and creates window of opportunity for system transformation. Notes Alberta's variety of school types (public, private, charter, religious, specialty arts/hockey) all open to residents. Emphasizes that most other services are similarly limited in small communities, so rural constraints shouldn't prevent choice. Well-reasoned argument with practical implementation suggestions.

April 2014 A Credentials losing market value Analysis of shifting market dynamics for educational credentials. Don argues that degrees and diplomas—historically… ▸ more
Analysis of shifting market dynamics for educational credentials. Don argues that degrees and diplomas—historically "blue chip stocks" of personal portfolios—are declining in value because they reflect readily available knowledge rather than demonstrated competence. Institutions have been protected monopolies with little incentive to innovate or compete. The emerging problem: credentials don't mirror community needs, are inflexible and front-loaded, expensive to produce, and haven't adapted to technology. Discusses alternative credentials ("knock-offs") and discerning consumers demanding better value. Written April 2014.

Don's working notes from April 2014 outlining mathematical structure and educational philosophy. Outlines steps (identify competencies, qualification levels, assessment instruments, qualification systems) and program elements (information, assessment, instruction, tutoring, clubs, competitions) while establishing conditions emphasizing individual participation and skill documentation.

Mar 26, 2014 14 Accountability_En and Fr The March 26, 2014 fourteenth issue, edited by Don Glendenning, distinguishes responsibility from accountability… ▸ more
The March 26, 2014 fourteenth issue, edited by Don Glendenning, distinguishes responsibility from accountability through columnist Arthur Sherren's framework. Don notes that employees are "responsible for their actions, behaviours, attitudes, decisions, work and performance" - they own and correct mistakes. Accountability represents "the process of making, keeping and managing promises and expectations… being liable or answerable for overall outcomes." Applying this to schools, Don argues teachers are responsible for ensuring students learn, while principals as instructional leaders are accountable for students gaining skills needed for postsecondary education or employment. Students and parents also bear responsibility for school success. The bilingual issue includes discussion of professional development, highlighting James Kempton's argument for teacher-controlled continuing education (100 hours annually) modeled on Royal College standards rather than school-mandated in-service days that research shows have limited impact. This issue demonstrates Don's sophisticated management thinking and commitment to clarity about educational roles.

Jan 20, 2014 11 PISA again The January 20, 2014 eleventh issue, edited by Don Glendenning, examines PISA (Program for International Student… ▸ more
The January 20, 2014 eleventh issue, edited by Don Glendenning, examines PISA (Program for International Student Assessment) results and their implications. Don cautions that while PISA provides useful feedback and international comparison, test results should not drive all educational decisions. He highlights an OECD survey of 11 participating countries showing that schools with greater autonomy tend to perform better on PISA. Critically, Don notes that when schools account publicly for results through achievement data, greater autonomy correlates with better performance, but without accountability arrangements, autonomous schools can perform worse. This balanced perspective reflects Don's sophisticated understanding of educational governance. The newsletter reminds readers of the January 20 workshop on youth preparedness and directs readers to OECD resources. Don recommends the Vancouver Sun article on British Columbia school report card reforms.

2014 D 37 Engagement A proposal to engage young Islanders in documenting significant community events and decisions from the past 150 years… ▸ more
A proposal to engage young Islanders in documenting significant community events and decisions from the past 150 years through interviews with seniors and examination of local records. Objectives include increasing community knowledge and pride, building intergenerational relationships, recognizing community builders, and strengthening communication skills. Don proposes organizing groups in fourteen communities with priority for 14-year-olds (honoring the year 2014). Implementation includes a part-time coordinator and local organizers with honoraria, community group funding for materials and events, and a province-wide gathering to share findings. Reports would be placed in libraries and schools, with originals in provincial archives. This initiative combines educational benefit, intergenerational connection, and community history preservation.

This initiative addresses a real gap—working youth's continued need for literacy support—and demonstrates Don's ability to design programs that fit within people's actual circumstances.

Sep 19, 2013 07 Helping someone learn The September 19, 2013 seventh issue, edited by Don Glendenning, presents practical guidance on supporting learners.… ▸ more
The September 19, 2013 seventh issue, edited by Don Glendenning, presents practical guidance on supporting learners. Don draws parallels between parental health support and educational support, arguing that intentional engagement in learning builds lifelong habits. He outlines concrete strategies: creating new learning opportunities, teaching new skills, providing practice opportunities, directing learners to relevant media, offering feedback, recognizing achievement, encouraging engagement, fostering dialogue about innovations, modeling active learning, promoting self-assessment, demonstrating procedures, and supporting discovery. The newsletter includes discussion of "The Secret Teacher" column's critique of data manipulation in education systems and addresses the "word gap" - research showing children from lower-income homes hear millions fewer words than affluent peers by age three. Don advocates for awareness of poverty's impact on achievement and highlights the Lena Research Foundation's digital language processor supporting parent-child interactions.

Jul 15, 2013 05 fundamental skills The July 15, 2013 fifth issue, edited by Don Glendenning, explores fundamental employability skills developed by The… ▸ more
The July 15, 2013 fifth issue, edited by Don Glendenning, explores fundamental employability skills developed by The Conference Board of Canada. Don outlines five core competency areas: communication (reading, writing, listening, sharing information using technology); information management (locating, gathering, organizing, analyzing knowledge across disciplines); numeracy (measuring, calculating, data recording); and problem-solving (assessing situations, seeking perspectives, identifying root causes, implementing and evaluating solutions). The newsletter discusses parents' frustration with curriculum outcome-based report cards and advocates for clearer parent communication. Don emphasizes that parents need specific information about learning problems to provide effective home support, and highlights the Atlantic Provinces' common outcome system available on provincial education websites. This issue demonstrates Don's commitment to accessible educational communication and parental engagement.

Jun 15, 2013 04-OBE The June 15, 2013 fourth issue provides Don Glendenning's guest editorial on Outcomes-Based Education (OBE), a… ▸ more
The June 15, 2013 fourth issue provides Don Glendenning's guest editorial on Outcomes-Based Education (OBE), a student-centered, results-oriented approach founded by sociologist William Spady. Don explains OBE's premise that all individuals can learn, with outcomes written for traditional, transitional, or transformational goals. He details learner-centered principles: clear identification of learning goals, progress based on demonstrated achievement, accommodation of individual needs through varied instructional strategies, and provision of time and assistance for students to reach potential. The newsletter also covers skill training reforms in Britain, year-round schooling innovations in Holland, and Project HIPPY. Don's thoughtful explanation of OBE reflects his engagement with contemporary educational research and commitment to student-centered pedagogy.

May 10, 2013 02 Elections The May 10, 2013 second issue of Open Education addresses school board elections as an educational issue central to… ▸ more
The May 10, 2013 second issue of Open Education addresses school board elections as an educational issue central to student learning. Don argues that student achievement improves when families, schools, and communities collaborate, citing National Parent Teacher Association research. He advocates for local decision-making authority for trustees, contending that low voter turnout reflects perceived lack of trustee power rather than community disinterest. The newsletter thanks Gaylene Smith for her account of teaching at College of the North Atlantic in Qatar and discusses debates over voting age and student board representation in British Columbia and PEI. This issue exemplifies Don's civic engagement advocacy and belief in democratic participation's role in educational improvement.

Apr 9, 2013 4 Reading Comprehensive April 9, 2013 synthesis of Don's reading education research and advocacy. Positions reading as golden… ▸ more
Comprehensive April 9, 2013 synthesis of Don's reading education research and advocacy. Positions reading as golden skill and tool for lifelong learning within education framework emphasizing individual potential development across life roles. Emphasizes reading as community problem requiring partnership approach beyond school boundaries. Catalogs extensive statistics on reading challenges, identifies performance levels from emerging pre-reader through expert reader, and details key performance criteria (comprehension, material variety, syntax, fluency, vocabulary, sound-decoding). Documents reading competencies, development strategies, benchmarks, and intervention points. Includes practical proposals: reading as separate report card subject, consistent standards, rating scales, flexible scheduling, summer/evening/Saturday classes, tutoring, reading clinics. Catalogs issues (emphasis gaps, funding continuity, jurisdiction divisions, specialist shortages, professional association absence, parent support gaps, reporting inconsistency). Proposes comprehensive improvement strategy incorporating information, engagement, innovation, recognition, research, early diagnosis, and modernized delivery. Reflects Don's commitment to making reading improvement systematic, community-wide effort.

March 2013 2-5 CBE Comprehensive notes on Outcome-Based Education (OBE) and Competency-Based Education (CBE) dated March 2013. Don… ▸ more
Comprehensive notes on Outcome-Based Education (OBE) and Competency-Based Education (CBE) dated March 2013. Don distinguishes between the two approaches and traces OBE's influence on curriculum development globally. He credits William Spady as the main proponent and examines definitions, outcomes at multiple levels (societal, community, school, program, course), and characteristics of effective OBE systems. The document outlines DACUM methodology, provides rating scales and performance levels, lists strengths (shifting focus from content to learner) and weaknesses (confusion between outcomes and levels), and includes Atlantic Canada's Essential Graduation Learnings and verb taxonomy aligned with Bloom's taxonomy. Reflects Don's systematic approach to educational philosophy and methodology.

Dated February 28, 2013, this paper documents reading through a learner rather than teacher perspective. Don emphasizes reading as the golden skill and tool for lifelong learning, positioning it as community problem requiring partnership approach. The paper provides comprehensive reading definitions and situates reading within broader communication contexts (listening, writing, speaking). Don creates detailed reading competency lists and performance criteria (range of material, comprehension, fluency, independence, speed, vocabulary, flow). He proposes custom rating scales reflecting public understanding of reading levels, from Level 6 (wide variety of complex materials) through Level 0 (no word recognition). Includes detailed appendix with reading competencies from North Carolina Conference of English Instructors covering comprehension, analysis, interpretation, language/style, and research competencies. Proposes practical activities (Ooka Island programs, tutor lists, common rating scales, reading clinics) and specific recommendations: replace outcomes with competencies, provide personal reading records, establish minimal performance levels, ensure consistent assessment criteria and benchmarks.

Jan 2, 2013 D 7 Student advocate Dated January 2, 2013, this proposal addresses the gap in educational support services for individuals outside formal… ▸ more
Dated January 2, 2013, this proposal addresses the gap in educational support services for individuals outside formal schooling. Don advocates for a community-based educational support service with a student advocate position—a professional independent from schools who would help people navigate educational choices and access programs. The service would operate like a "family doctor" for education matters, providing information, counsel, advice, assessment, monitoring, and referral. The advocate would require education experience, occupational background outside education, strong communication skills, and maturity. This innovative model would serve community members of all ages seeking to improve their education.

2013 Map Comprehensive proposal and background document for creating a competency-based mathematics curriculum map for PEI.… ▸ more
Comprehensive proposal and background document for creating a competency-based mathematics curriculum map for PEI. Begins with 2013 CFPEI youth survey findings regarding preparedness gaps, followed by Education 20/20's multi-year exploration of "basic skills." Document articulates major concerns: undefined content/levels, economic drag from low skill levels, duplication of remedial teaching, scattered institutional responsibility, and low priority given to basic skills instruction. Proposes using the 1978 "Practical Mathematics" analysis as a foundation and DACUM methodology (developed by Nova Scotia NewStart, pioneered by Holland College) for creating a shared competency framework. Outlines next steps: establish steering committee, review existing analysis, develop rating scales, identify profiles, and develop assessment processes.

May 25, 2012 AA-3 26-5 Interventions School autonomy Major policy document on school governance based on Don's Indiana University assignment analyzing university governance… ▸ more
Major policy document on school governance based on Don's Indiana University assignment analyzing university governance through Kant's philosophy. Defines governance as "who makes what decisions, and in what manner." Highlights confusion between education and schooling, credentials vs. qualifications, and professional accountability. Presents a case from OECD research showing direct correlation between school autonomy and student performance. Proposes decision-making framework: learners/parents choose schools, teachers manage individual learners, principals lead instruction, trustees/superintendent operate schools, Deputy Minister ensures quality, Minister delegates operational roles. References Alberta school board decisions and proposes immediate PEI changes including consolidating educational responsibilities and delegating to lowest appropriate levels. Includes section on "Thoughts on School Governance" (May 25, 2012 draft) emphasizing freedom to teach with accountability to those served.

c. 2011 Flipped classrooms Don's analysis of flipped classroom pedagogy emerging circa 2011. Documents example of advanced calculus teacher… ▸ more
Don's analysis of flipped classroom pedagogy emerging circa 2011. Documents example of advanced calculus teacher (Stacey Roshan) using digitally recorded home-viewing lectures to enable classroom discussion. Identifies three keys to success: students completing homework, quality of lessons (depth and methodology), and production quality. Discusses trade-offs between Dr. Scott Morris's advice to prioritize content speed over perfection versus Don's position that week-by-week delivery demands professional production. Emphasizes importance of engaging delivery: "if a lesson in class isn't engaging, you might still hold attention through classroom presence; a boring home lesson faces hundreds of distractions." Reflects that educators should maintain same quality standards for students as expected from students. Includes references to David Truss, Palmer Academy (Detroit), Promethean, TED talks. Demonstrates Don's continued engagement with emerging educational technologies and methodologies in later career.

2011 Challenge for credit Minister's Directive 2011-01 establishing External Credentialing Policy for PEI high schools. Policy allows students… ▸ more
Minister's Directive 2011-01 establishing External Credentialing Policy for PEI high schools. Policy allows students (grades 10-12) to earn high school credits for external programs, courses, and assessments meeting or exceeding provincial standards. Key provisions include: maximum one non-compulsory credit via external credential toward 20-credit (English) or 25-credit (French) graduation requirement; external credentials cannot fulfill compulsory credits; equivalency restrictions apply; external credentials must contribute to Essential Graduation Learnings (aesthetic expression, citizenship, communication, personal development, problem solving, technological competence); students pay costs and fees; schools verify credentials and maintain records; department maintains approved provider list; External Credentialing Advisory Committee monitors implementation. Document includes detailed requirements for program providers regarding organizational profile, course profile, essential graduation learnings, and principles of learning. This represents significant curriculum flexibility policy allowing recognition of learning in multiple contexts.

This document presents general notes on educational innovation, specifically referencing Bishop Carroll High School in Calgary, Alberta. 2009 2009-03-New Directors A detailed January 23, 2009 analysis for the Nominating Committee on recruitment needs. Don identifies four core… ▸ more
A detailed January 23, 2009 analysis for the Nominating Committee on recruitment needs. Don identifies four core foundation roles (endowment building, grant-making, community leadership, small business management) and proposes specific new director qualities addressing identified gaps. He advocates recruiting from private sector, recruiting more women and younger people (40-60), and seeking individuals reflecting emerging PEI demographics (technology, manufacturing, youth, aboriginals, immigrants). His specific assessments of individual candidates reveal both shrewd judgment and knowledge of the PEI professional community. Don emphasizes proper orientation, role clarification, and early assignment of responsibilities to engage new directors meaningfully.

2009 2009-Appendix Guidelines appendix for the Catherine Callbeck Legacy Fund establishing how grants should be directed. The appendix… ▸ more
Guidelines appendix for the Catherine Callbeck Legacy Fund establishing how grants should be directed. The appendix defines literacy broadly to include reading, writing, oral communications, numeracy, problem-solving, and computer skills. Importantly, it focuses funding on adults who have left the formal education system or haven't yet entered it, supports specific initiatives rather than ongoing operational costs, encourages partnerships and team-based approaches, and explicitly excludes research funding. These guidelines reflect Don's pragmatic philosophy of targeted, action-oriented literacy improvement.

January 2008 Poverty proposal January 2008 proposal exploring poverty alleviation initiatives in Eastern Prince Edward Island. Don's research… ▸ more
January 2008 proposal exploring poverty alleviation initiatives in Eastern Prince Edward Island. Don's research identified multiple intervention opportunities: expanding Breakfast for Learning program (serving 5,500 students at $0.85/day), enhancing school poverty funds for clothing and field trip assistance, supporting isolated high school students living independently, and addressing gaps in Christmas basket distribution systems. Proposes three management approaches: direct grants to existing programs, call for proposals, or community committee implementation. Demonstrates Don's fieldwork approach to needs assessment and his interest in identifying gaps between existing services and vulnerable populations.

Early planning minutes from November 15, 2006 Agricultural Heritage Project meeting with Dr. Tom Hall, Willie Dunbar, and Weldon Rennie. Mar 20, 2005 1-Not the Fault of Teachers An essay written March 20, 2005 (last updated December 14, 2005) in which Don defends teachers against systemic… ▸ more
An essay written March 20, 2005 (last updated December 14, 2005) in which Don defends teachers against systemic education shortcomings. He argues that public complaints about education—rigidity, lack of relevance, poor standards—are unfairly attributed to teachers when nearly half the education budget goes to non-salary items and system-level decisions constrain individual teacher autonomy. Don advocates for system-level changes from senior leadership (ministers and deputies) to empower teachers as professionals and improve educational resources available to learners.

February 2005 07 CHOICE AS AN AGENT OF CHANGE Written from Panama City Beach, Florida in February 2005, Don advocates school choice as an educational reform… ▸ more
Written from Panama City Beach, Florida in February 2005, Don advocates school choice as an educational reform mechanism. He notes that consumers choose churches, doctors, lawyers, and mayors but have limited school choice and no teacher choice. He argues choice increases product range, competitive pricing, and service quality—principles applicable to education. Don proposes four intervention points: school choice, teacher choice, flexible study time (including part-time and summer), and exams on demand rather than schedule. He emphasizes that choice empowers learners and increases accountability while removing excuses for non-performance.

2005 Teacher-led Schools Don's analysis of emerging teacher-led school movement, featuring case study of Palmer Park Preparatory Academy… ▸ more
Don's analysis of emerging teacher-led school movement, featuring case study of Palmer Park Preparatory Academy (Detroit, K-8, principal-less, union-member teachers). Identifies significance: teachers as professionals willing to be accountable; union concerned with education quality not just working conditions; school boards confident in experimental models. Other examples: Boston Teachers Union School (K-8, 2005), Minnesota EdVisions charter network (1990s), Math and Science Leadership Academy Denver (union-designed, team coverage, peer evaluation, individual attention), Milwaukee teacher cooperatives running dozen schools. Notes two management models: MSLA model with one/two managing teachers as partners; Law Firm model with manager reporting to partners' committee. Cites Education Evolving finding that teacher-led schools show higher morale, less turnover, greater improvement motivation—analogous to business ownership versus employment. Sources education journalist Stacy Teicher Khadaroo (Christian Science Monitor), Walt Gardner (Education Week), and online resources. Demonstrates Don's continuing engagement with innovative governance and delivery models even in retirement years.

May 4, 2004 Educational Bureaucracy A critical analysis of educational bureaucracy, written May 4, 2004. Glendenning catalogs the extensive control systems… ▸ more
A critical analysis of educational bureaucracy, written May 4, 2004. Glendenning catalogs the extensive control systems schools maintain: determining students' departure times, bus behavior, lunch schedules, subject choices, room assignments, exam timing, and departure times. Teachers face comparable controls. He argues schools operate on obsolete industrial organizational models while other sectors have evolved. The document advocates learner-centered education beginning with educational assessment, permanent educational advisors (like family doctors), and individual learning plans—contrasting this with current group-based instruction assuming same-age students need identical lessons. He criticizes both bureaucratic control and the lack of individual attention in mass education.

1998 Post sec- Sports & Rec Sam Dillon reports on trends showing American colleges spending more on recreational facilities and administration… ▸ more
Sam Dillon reports on trends showing American colleges spending more on recreational facilities and administration while proportionally reducing expenditure on instruction. Study by Delta Cost Project analyzed spending 1998-2008, documenting a shift toward "country-clubization" of universities. Private research universities increased spending on instruction 22% over the decade, but 36% for student services and 36% for institutional support. Community colleges show similar patterns. The article criticizes this reallocation as inconsistent with educational mission and notes the economic crisis has made spending models unsustainable.

1995 Writing Six-level writing assessment rubric establishing performance criteria from disorganized/many errors (level 1) through… ▸ more
Six-level writing assessment rubric establishing performance criteria from disorganized/many errors (level 1) through creatively written with acceptable standards (level 6). Each level describes sentence structure, grammar, vocabulary appropriateness, and organization. Level 6: creative writing with acceptable grammar, sentence structure, organization. Level 5: organized with precise vocabulary, good sentence structure, appropriate grammar, some creativity. Level 4: organized with few errors. Level 3: acceptable sentence structure, grammar, vocabulary. Level 2: limited vocabulary, significant grammar/structure errors. Level 1: disorganized, many errors, limited vocabulary. Document cites Information House 1995 source. Reflects competency-based assessment framework consistent with Don's educational philosophy of defining observable performance levels.

Don identifies critical issues: computer technology implementation, teacher role transformation, accountability systems, educational leadership, serving disadvantaged students, and inter-level coordination. Aug 22, 1990 Outcome Based Education Comprehensive educational document examining Outcome-Based Education (OBE) principles. Based on Spady's definition of… ▸ more
Comprehensive educational document examining Outcome-Based Education (OBE) principles. Based on Spady's definition of OBE: "clearly focusing and organizing everything in an educational system around what is essential for all students to be able to do successfully at the end of their learning experiences." Document outlines Spady's four essential OBE principles, discusses clarity of focus, high expectations for all students, and the importance of designing curriculum backward from desired outcomes. Includes EXIT OUTCOMES from Michigan (adopted August 22, 1990) and Atlantic Canada Essential Graduation Learnings. Details process elements including teacher-directed vs. self-directed learning, formal/informal, competitive/consensual approaches. Emphasizes student-centered, results-oriented, standards-referenced systems.

1987 CRSP Glendenning's retrospective on designing CRSP, a competency-based secondary school model combining personalized,… ▸ more
Glendenning's retrospective on designing CRSP, a competency-based secondary school model combining personalized, competency-based, and computer-based components with flexible structures and site-based management. Developed in his post-retirement consulting work (after 1987), the model was designed to be an alternative to "lock-step, teacher-centered, classroom-based delivery." The document describes major shifts embodied in CRSP: from fixed admission schedules to flexible entry/exit; from time-bound to competency-based curriculum; from graded to non-graded continuous progress; from teacher-centered to learner-centered; and from percentage-based to competency-based reporting. Glendenning claims it's "the only model which includes all components of a competency based system."

1969 76 school Assessment,,, Comprehensive article by Don on school assessment, defining three grading elements (formative assessment/feedback,… ▸ more
Comprehensive article by Don on school assessment, defining three grading elements (formative assessment/feedback, summative evaluation, and ranking) based on Robert Paul Wolff's 1969 "A Discourse on Grading." Don argues that assessing for proficiency assignment and ranking may conflict with teaching functions, citing examples from apprenticeship, International Baccalaureate, and qualification authorities where teaching and grading are separated. He proposes using "Level" terminology rather than "Grade" to distinguish achievement levels from administrative groupings, addressing student, parent, and teacher stress about testing and grading.

1969 01 Standards - the current challenge Don reflects on his leadership journey at Holland College with forty-year perspective. Upon founding Holland College in… ▸ more
Don reflects on his leadership journey at Holland College with forty-year perspective. Upon founding Holland College in 1969, he identified institutional flexibility as education's most pressing issue, addressing how educational systems had locked themselves into teaching models failing to reflect learning science. He describes Holland College's adoption and development of the DACUM model from Nova Scotia NewStart Corporation, valuing its learner-centered approach, relevant competencies, community interaction, cost-effectiveness, performance-based rating, continuing education tools, learner-teacher communication, and flexibility. Writing forty years later, Don identifies exit standards as the urgent contemporary challenge—a gap between public expectations and system delivery. He cites Martin Haberman's research on the non-academic gap between school and employment. Don emphasizes the need for performance-based standards with clear exit outcomes, well-defined performance level descriptions, and recognized benchmarks, noting progress but indicating work remains.

1968 Worth A Look An analysis of three major educational models: DACUM (Design A Curriculum, a Canadian competency identification… ▸ more
An analysis of three major educational models: DACUM (Design A Curriculum, a Canadian competency identification methodology from 1968), CRSP (Canadian Restructured School Plan developed during Don's consulting work), and NZQA (New Zealand's national qualifications framework). Don credits DACUM's strength to its practitioner-based approach and escape from academic semantics in favor of life-based, occupation-specific competencies. He describes CRSP as providing personalized, competency-based, and computer-assisted learning within flexible structures. NZQA's framework demonstrates how to recognize learning through multiple pathways while maintaining consistent standards. Don advocates for adopting these models despite barriers like terminology confusion and educators' preference for current systems, as they represent significant advances in learner-centered, performance-based education.

1956 Levels A-4 Bloom Revised This document presents Bloom's Revised Taxonomy, a classification framework for learning objectives updated by Lorin… ▸ more
This document presents Bloom's Revised Taxonomy, a classification framework for learning objectives updated by Lorin Anderson from Benjamin Bloom's original 1956 work. The six levels, from lowest to highest order thinking, are: (1) Remembering - recall and recognition; (2) Understanding - comprehension and translation; (3) Applying - using concepts in new situations; (4) Analyzing - breaking down materials into components; (5) Evaluating - making judgments about value; (6) Creating - building new structures from elements. Each level includes specific action verbs and practical examples relevant to workplace and educational contexts. This framework is foundational to instructional design, assessment, and educational outcomes specification.

1912 98 Skill training Don laments the decline of quality skill training in PEI, tracing history back to the 1912 Agricultural Aid Act and… ▸ more
Don laments the decline of quality skill training in PEI, tracing history back to the 1912 Agricultural Aid Act and noting how federal leadership maintained standards through stringent requirements. He observes that for fifty years PVI and PCVHS provided entry-level work training that often launched small business entrepreneurs, but this gap persists with limited employment training now available. Quoting Herbert Spencer on how social needs have historically subordinated individual needs in education, he questions why skill development training is not available on PEI and calls for official response, emphasizing that practical work-ready education remains essential to community economic development.

Undated Levels of education Glendenning's critical analysis distinguishing between education and schooling - a recurring theme in his work. Defines… ▸ more
Glendenning's critical analysis distinguishing between education and schooling - a recurring theme in his work. Defines education as "any experience that has a formative effect on the way a person thinks feels or acts" while schooling is "that part of one's education resulting from experiences planned and organized while in an educational institution." Critiques PEI Department of Education for focusing exclusively on children in school, suggesting this should be titled "Department of Public Instruction" instead. Advocates for provincial education systems that address all citizens' learning needs across the lifespan. Argues that distinguishing education from schooling clarifies communication, expands learning opportunities, and reinforces lifelong learning. - **Key People**: None specifically named - **Key Organizations**: PEI Department of Education; references New Brunswick and Nova Scotia education systems - **Tags**: PROFESSIONAL, EDUCATION, MANUSCRIPT - **Eulogy Relevance**: HIGH - Shows Glendenning's fundamental critique and reconceptualization of education vs. schooling, a core theme throughout his consulting work.

Specialized rating scale for mathematics education focusing on six higher-order cognitive dimensions: Evaluation (verify answers and select optimal results), Communication (represent information symbolically/graphically), Synthesis (generalize patterns to principles), Analysis (interpret mathematical models), Application (use multiple solution methods), and Criteria (apply mathematical reasoning). Undated Issues-3, Schooling-1 This lengthy document explores systemic issues in schooling, treating issues as complex problems requiring deep… ▸ more
This lengthy document explores systemic issues in schooling, treating issues as complex problems requiring deep analysis. Don distinguishes between issues, symptoms, and incidents, and provides methodology for issue analysis (who, what, when, where, why). He addresses five major issues: Focus on Knowledge (learner-centered versus subject-centered instruction), Professional Freedom (autonomy and accountability), Delivery Model (administrative versus educational functions), Assessment and Standards (independence from teaching), and Credentials and Qualifications. The paper advocates for learner-centered approaches, teacher autonomy in instructional decisions, separation of coaching from referee functions (grading/ranking), outcome-based education, and independent assessment bodies. Implementation strategies include public campaigns, school autonomy, community advisory boards, and learner-focused policies.

Undated Levels 1 This document addresses the measurement and reporting of educational achievement levels, distinguishing between… ▸ more
This document addresses the measurement and reporting of educational achievement levels, distinguishing between age-based grades and actual proficiency levels. Don critiques current PEI educational practices where learners at different starting points study the same material for fixed periods while learning at different rates—making grade-based achievement reporting confusing. He argues that assessment functions should be independent of those who teach, certify proficiency regardless of age, and establish levels rather than rankings. The document proposes elements including comprehensive competency analysis, evaluative criteria, benchmarks, criterion-referenced rating scales, and recognizable proficiency levels. He references examples like the Apprenticeship Red Seal program, Canadian Language Benchmarks, Red Cross Swim Program, and Royal Canadian Conservatory of Music as successful independent assessment models.

Undated Levels 3 This occasional paper addresses educational standards and proficiency measurement. Don reiterates that while many… ▸ more
This occasional paper addresses educational standards and proficiency measurement. Don reiterates that while many education improvement suggestions focus on early childhood, teacher training, and engagement, standards revision is often overlooked. Key issues include: establishing levels based on subject examination rather than learner age, distinguishing between administrative grade levels and achievement levels, separating teaching from student ranking/sorting, establishing clear benchmarks, and developing published rating scales. He proposes characteristics for assessment: independent from teachers, certifying proficiency, open to all ages/backgrounds, non-competitive, confidential. Concrete examples include Red Seal apprenticeship, Canadian Language Benchmarks, Red Cross Swim Program, and Royal Canadian Conservatory of Music—all models where teaching and certification are separated.

Undated Levels-4 This brief document challenges conventional education level measurement, arguing that years of formal study and… ▸ more
This brief document challenges conventional education level measurement, arguing that years of formal study and credential accumulation are not meaningful measures of education. It proposes determining educational levels based on: what a person knows or can do, and the level of proficiency demonstrated in each competency area. Don references leadership, communication skills, and languages as examples and presents a DACUM-based model describing six discernible levels: exercising leadership in life roles; functioning well in roles with special abilities; functioning adequately with minimal support; functioning with assistance/direction; and inability to function without or with assistance. This represents a shift from traditional grading to competency-based proficiency descriptions.

Undated OBE paper A detailed analysis of Outcomes Based Education philosophy and implementation, contrasting traditional content-focused… ▸ more
A detailed analysis of Outcomes Based Education philosophy and implementation, contrasting traditional content-focused instruction with learner-centered, outcomes-driven approaches. The paper examines William Spady's OBE paradigm, including three premises (all students can learn, successful learning promotes more learning, schools control success conditions) and four power principles (clarity of focus, expanded support, high expectations, design-down approach). Don critically assesses both strengths (consistency, accountability, accessibility) and weaknesses (lack of standard models, grade-level confusion, teacher autonomy concerns) of OBE implementation. He reviews exit outcomes from Saranac Lake schools and Atlantic regional outcomes, ultimately recommending renewal of outcome identification processes and development of implementation handbooks—demonstrating his evidence-based, reform-minded approach.

Undated learning diagram A visual flowchart organizing learning into three phases: planning, engaging, and documenting. The planning phase… ▸ more
A visual flowchart organizing learning into three phases: planning, engaging, and documenting. The planning phase includes attempting tasks, gathering information, seeking advice, observing others, undertaking assessment, and developing learning plans. The engaging phase encompasses attempting tasks, receiving instruction, gathering information, seeking advice, receiving feedback, observing others, using resources, and applying new learning. The documenting phase involves transcripts, credentials, and qualifications. This diagram represents Don's effort to create a structured model showing learning as a dynamic, iterative process with clear entry/exit points and documentation procedures, reflecting his systems-design approach to educational reform.

Undated 500 CRSP Don describes his most ambitious consulting project: designing the Canadian Restructured School Plan to provide an… ▸ more
Don describes his most ambitious consulting project: designing the Canadian Restructured School Plan to provide an alternative to lock-step, teacher-centered classroom systems. The model emphasizes personalization, competency-based content drawn from real life, computer-assisted delivery, flexible structure, and site-based management. CRSP tracks students from entry through exit based on competency mastery rather than time served. The methodology included a national steering committee, literature review, focus groups, visits to innovative schools across Canada, and consultation with 40 educational leaders. The model identifies major shifts from time-bound to flexible curricula, graded to continuous progress, and teacher-centered to learner-centered instruction.

Undated 500 DACUM Don provides comprehensive analysis of DACUM (Design A Curriculum), a Canadian innovation for identifying competencies… ▸ more
Don provides comprehensive analysis of DACUM (Design A Curriculum), a Canadian innovation for identifying competencies in occupations and fields. He credits its rigor, relevance, low cost, and internal consistency. DACUM's strength lies in consulting practitioners and presenting competencies on a single sheet in language practitioners understand—life-based rather than academically abstract. Don praises Adams' performance-based rating scale with benchmarks and competency-based criteria. He describes DACUM's applications in resource planning, prior learning recognition, career planning, and certification. However, wider adoption suffers because the acronym includes "curriculum" (DACUM is a planning tool, not curriculum), its origins in adult education rather than academic settings, its shift of control from teacher to learner, and its demand for more rigorous rating scales than traditional schools employ.

Undated 500 If the Learner Hasn Don reflects on his teaching journey and the moment when his understanding of the learning-teaching process… ▸ more
Don reflects on his teaching journey and the moment when his understanding of the learning-teaching process crystallized. He recalls being a teacher before understanding pedagogy deeply, attributing this partly to his circumstances: small high school background, brief six-month initial training, youth, and one-room schoolhouse experience. Later, through formal study and varied professional experiences, his understanding deepened. Don describes using the provocative statement "If the learner hasn't learned, the teacher hasn't taught" in teacher education settings, which animated debate. He defends this principle against objections by noting that while learning may occur in groups, it is inherently individual—teachers must succeed with each learner, not groups.

Undated 500 Not a competition Don argues schools should not operate on competitive principles despite teaching competition. He advocates for… ▸ more
Don argues schools should not operate on competitive principles despite teaching competition. He advocates for individual learning progress without public grade posting or student ranking, which he views as sorting rather than educating. Don contends that reaching an "A" level is achievable for most students with additional time, questioning why schools artificially limit achievement through time constraints rather than allowing flexible completion. He critiques schools for penalizing students by forcing them to advance before achieving mastery. Notably, he reports that over 50 percent of US high schools had discontinued ranking by the time of writing. Don's own memory of a grade 8-9 ranking ceremony lasting impression, prompting lifelong reflection on its impact. He advocates discontinuing ranking and redirecting resources toward raising performance levels for all students.

Undated 92- Challenge for Credit Don proposes that all schools, colleges, and universities offering courses for credit should allow students to… ▸ more
Don proposes that all schools, colleges, and universities offering courses for credit should allow students to challenge for credit without formal enrollment. Educational institutions already assess prior learning (especially experiential), award advanced standing, and allow some students to skip grades. Don's personal experiences include exemption from a foreign language course and direct university entry from Grade 11. His proposal extends these practices universally. He argues this is crucial because society is "driven by credentials" available primarily through educational institutions, yet individuals acquire knowledge outside formal enrollment. Allowing challenge examinations would enable students to validate learning from any source—work experience, self-study, non-traditional training—and obtain essential credentials.

Undated A 1 Going to School or Getting an Education A foundational piece distinguishing between schooling and education, fundamental to Don's educational philosophy. He… ▸ more
A foundational piece distinguishing between schooling and education, fundamental to Don's educational philosophy. He contrasts schooling (formal institutional training) with education (broader development of abilities and positive behavior values in society). Don uses educational dictionary definitions and references George Bernard Shaw and Canadian business leaders to illustrate the confusion between these concepts. He argues that education encompasses learning from home, church, community, and experience—not just schools. The essay explores why credentialing systems emphasize degrees over actual capabilities, and proposes that education should be measured by one's ability to apply knowledge, demonstrate citizenship, communicate effectively, solve problems, and maintain ethical responsibility rather than by institutions attended or credentials held.

Undated A 2 The Teaching Learning Process Drawing on his early teaching experience in a one-room rural school at age seventeen, Don reflects on the critical gap… ▸ more
Drawing on his early teaching experience in a one-room rural school at age seventeen, Don reflects on the critical gap in his teacher training regarding the teaching-learning process. He emphasizes three components: learner, teacher, and process. Don rejects the "empty vessel" metaphor for learners, arguing instead that teachers create conditions for effective learning. He defines learning as behavioral change across cognitive, affective, and psychomotor domains. A key insight is that "a teacher hasn't taught if a learner hasn't learned," applied to each individual learner. Don advocates for schools to develop and publish learning models showing the steps learners take from course entry to exit, with multiple pathways accounting for different starting points and paces. He provides practical guidance for drafting such models and argues this understanding would have made him a better teacher.

Undated A 3 A Snow Job Don critiques blanket school closures during winter storms as administratively convenient but educationally disruptive… ▸ more
Don critiques blanket school closures during winter storms as administratively convenient but educationally disruptive and disrespectful of parental authority. He observes that PEI weather is unpredictable and that many students walk or are driven to school and could attend safely despite storms. Don argues that schools should remain open and parents should decide whether their children attend based on individual circumstances and information. He identifies the various stakeholders (parents, bus drivers, school facilities staff, teachers) and their respective responsibilities. Critically, he notes that treating all students as identical ignores the reality that students operate at different levels and learn at different paces. Don concludes that school closures reflect an inability to accommodate individual differences rather than genuine safety necessity.

Undated A Learning About Learning An extended essay in which Don reflects on his early teacher training and the absence of teaching-learning process… ▸ more
An extended essay in which Don reflects on his early teacher training and the absence of teaching-learning process instruction. He emphasizes that learning models should focus on learner activities, not teacher activities. Don advocates that schools adopt published learning models as part of program planning and teacher education—finding most schools lack them despite their fundamental importance. He provides a step-by-step guide for drafting an "ideal" learning model: marking entry and exit points, mapping all steps and their sequences, including optional and required components, and identifying alternate pathways. Don asks critical questions during model development: What steps should students take? In what sequence? How do we know? He notes that revising learning models throughout one's career reflects growing knowledge and experience, and expresses regret that he didn't understand this process earlier in his teaching career.

Undated AQUA (2) A proposal document outlining AQUA (Atlantic Provinces Qualification Authority), an independent organization designed… ▸ more
A proposal document outlining AQUA (Atlantic Provinces Qualification Authority), an independent organization designed to assess and certify performance-based qualifications separate from educational delivery. Don conceptualizes AQUA as addressing the problem that acquiring education and certifying qualifications are conflated but should be separate functions. AQUA would operate on a fee-for-service basis, accept direct applications or referrals, issue certificates based on demonstrated performance, and provide information on competency gaps. The proposed benefits include removing barriers to certification, focusing certification on performance rather than time served, encouraging self-study, allowing educational providers to focus on teaching while separating certification functions, and eliminating length of learning time as a certification component. Implementation would involve feasibility study, business planning, pilot testing, and full service launch.

Undated Assessing Student Performance A reflective account of Holland College's innovative STEP (Systematic Training and Education Program) approach to… ▸ more
A reflective account of Holland College's innovative STEP (Systematic Training and Education Program) approach to student performance assessment. Don credits Robert Adams of Nova Scotia NewStart Corporation with developing the DACUM-based performance rating scale, distinguishing it from traditional school evaluation. The essay outlines seven principles of expert supervisory judgment that informed DACUM development: supervisors sense satisfaction through simultaneous factors; they assess by observation and subjective judgment; competent supervisors recognize good work; they avoid single-instance judgments; they base recognition on demonstrated performance, not knowledge; they observe problem-solving processes; and they don't require rigid criteria. Don describes Holland College's implementation: learners self-rate upon entry and throughout, engage in learning plans with instructors, receive confirmation ratings, and understand greatest learning occurs when ratings change. He credits instructors' occupational backgrounds for understanding this performance-based approach, establishing a best practice for competency-based training.

Undated Assessment Based College (2) Don proposes "Canada College," an assessment-based institution addressing fragmentation of lifelong learning… ▸ more
Don proposes "Canada College," an assessment-based institution addressing fragmentation of lifelong learning credentials. He notes schools no longer monopolize education delivery; employers, unions, professional associations, and others provide training through various channels. Lifelong learners accumulate certificates from multiple sources but lack unified recognition. Don observes educational institutions resist crediting education dissimilar to their offerings or even their own extension courses. He proposes Canada College as a credit bank rather than educational provider, aggregating learning from schools, universities, colleges, work, self-study, seminars, and community activities. References include the Open Learning Agency in British Columbia and New Zealand's Qualification Authority model. Don suggests his own experience—building bridges, drainage systems, road work—could be assessed toward civil engineering credentials through portfolio submission. The proposal addresses time-based credit limitations, advocating for performance-based alternatives.

Undated Barriers to reform A concise outline identifying seven fundamental barriers to educational reform: limited view equating schooling with… ▸ more
A concise outline identifying seven fundamental barriers to educational reform: limited view equating schooling with education; prescribing education into rigid degrees, diplomas, and courses; monopolistic institutional control; time-based rather than performance-based frameworks; organization designed for industrial era production; overemphasis on qualifications over actual learning; and systems incapable of self-correction. This outline distills Don's critique of conventional educational systems into its essential components, suggesting his systematic analysis of why reform remains difficult. The brevity and clarity of this list indicate it may have served as foundational thinking for his more extensive reform proposals including AQUA and Canada College.
