 Read · Download
Undated 02 The Incubator Don conceptualizes schools as incubators preparing young people for adult life roles. Drawing from the Oregon Education… ▸ more
Don conceptualizes schools as incubators preparing young people for adult life roles. Drawing from the Oregon Education Act's six life roles (individual, learner, producer, consumer, family member, citizen), he argues all people benefit at some point from instructional support. He identifies the fundamental challenge: while schools fill important roles, society hasn't clearly expressed expectations about their function. Don contrasts business models (investment/consumer purchase) with education, which involves overlapping stakeholder interests (learners, service providers, investors, employers). He identifies a critical gap: educators measure quality by inputs (subjects, schooling years, institutions, instructor qualifications, technology, test scores) while taxpayers and employers judge by outputs—what graduates can do. Schools measure input and infer performance; the public rates performance directly. Don argues little progress in raising standards will occur until these conflicting measurement frameworks are reconciled, requiring dialogue that aligns training program content with community standards.

Undated 03 Teachers as Change Agents Don defines teaching fundamentally as helping others change across cognitive, affective, and psychomotor domains.… ▸ more
Don defines teaching fundamentally as helping others change across cognitive, affective, and psychomotor domains. Teachers shape lives and help learners develop new attitudes, skills, and knowledge. Don emphasizes learning is individual—only the learner can learn—and teaching creates change conditions. He reiterates his core principle: "A teacher hasn't taught if a learner hasn't learned," applied to each learner. Teachers stimulate learning through varied techniques: modeling, information provision, questioning, timelines, projects, field trips, and performance assessment. The crux of good teaching is creating favorable learning environments for each individual within group settings. Don notes schools organize around academic disciplines with assigned hours and teachers hired accordingly, resulting in report cards with subject ratings disconnected from life roles except future academic study. The essay implies this structure inadequately prepares learners for their actual future roles as individuals, learners, producers, consumers, family members, and citizens. The essay suggests a fundamental need to align schooling structures with adult life role preparation.

This document establishes the framework for competency-based assessment.

Undated 07 Components A technical but accessible exposition of the four components comprising a true educational standard: competencies… ▸ more
A technical but accessible exposition of the four components comprising a true educational standard: competencies (observable, measurable, community-drawn actions people can perform); criteria (quality measures like consistency, speed, safety); levels of performance (distinguishing how well someone performs, not just whether they can); and benchmarks (community-based reference points anchoring performance levels to real-world contexts). Glendenning critiques the vagueness in existing educational standards and provides his own "Glendenning version," arguing that effective standards must integrate all four components. He uses practical examples like editing or driving to illustrate how performance levels vary independently from basic competence.

Extensive research notes synthesizing literature on performance-based assessment and competency analysis. Glendenning documents the shift from input-based to outcome-based education, noting that "certificates and diplomas should be awarded based on demonstrable skills rather than hours of instruction." He explores performance assessment's advantages over traditional testing: better validity, more useful feedback to unsuccessful candidates, improved knowledge assessment, and potential cost-effectiveness. He emphasizes that "informed professional judgment is essential" to reliable assessment and advocates for competency analysis as the foundation for certification standards. The notes reveal Don's deep engagement with educational research and his conviction that better assessment drives educational improvement.

Glendenning's examination of educational vouchers as a market mechanism to improve education through competition and consumer choice. He finds the concept intriguing—students as consumers holding schools accountable through their funding choices and ability to leave. However, he notes vouchers remain experimental with inconclusive effectiveness data. He critiques limiting vouchers to poorly performing students, which stigmatizes the initiative. A genuine test, he argues, should provide vouchers universally, allowing all learners to vote with their feet regarding schools' effectiveness. The document reflects Don's faith in market mechanisms and consumer power as educational improvement levers.

Undated Final Exams Published as "MY 200 WORDS" commentary, Glendenning questions the term "final exams," suggesting it contradicts… ▸ more
Published as "MY 200 WORDS" commentary, Glendenning questions the term "final exams," suggesting it contradicts lifelong learning ideology. He distinguishes between exams for learning (diagnostic, improving) and exams for qualification (certifying competence), arguing only the latter belong outside schools. Schools should assess ongoing learning; external qualification authorities should determine competence for employment or further study. He advocates making validation available to lifelong learners, so people acquiring skills outside school can have learning recognized. The document exemplifies Don's precision in educational terminology and his concern for validating learning wherever it occurs.

Undated GRADUATION RATES Glendenning critiques schools' time-based structure and advocates making graduation the objective while time becomes… ▸ more
Glendenning critiques schools' time-based structure and advocates making graduation the objective while time becomes the variable. He notes schools measure achievement by how much students learn in fixed time, like measuring how far runners go in set time rather than how long they take to complete a course. Since learning rates vary individually and most students can master content with adequate time, extending the school year for struggling students would increase graduation rates. The proposal is modest—extra weeks for non-proficient students—but reflects his fundamental belief that time shouldn't constrain capability. The document demonstrates Don's capacity to identify simple, high-leverage systemic changes.

Undated Issues in secondary educ Don's contracted paper on critical secondary education issues in Canada, which became the foundation for the Canadian… ▸ more
Don's contracted paper on critical secondary education issues in Canada, which became the foundation for the Canadian Restructured School Plan. Working with a former university president, superintendent, and deputy minister, Don's group assumed education determines economic prosperity, technological change impacts schools, and secondary education must serve all youth in grades 10-12. The paper identifies six critical issues: information technology application, teacher preparation, accountability, school leadership, poorly served students, and inter-level coordination. Fourteen demonstration projects are proposed, ranging from technology implementation to teacher training models. This work was foundational to CRSP initiatives.

Undated Teacher Pay (172 words):** Don advocates substantial teacher salary increases while critiquing current pay-grid systems based on… ▸ more
(172 words):** Don advocates substantial teacher salary increases while critiquing current pay-grid systems based on training duration and tenure. Statistics Canada data show teacher salaries lag comparable professions. Don argues the grid system reflects what teachers know, not what they do, perpetuating low compensation. He proposes performance-based systems incorporating student numbers served, specialty complexity, responsibility, innovation, and market factors. Teachers should earn merit rewards for strong performance while facing market consequences for weak performance. However, Don notes teachers' limited autonomy—scripted curricula, prescribed timing, limited creativity—undermines professional status necessary for higher pay. He advocates professional control over working conditions as prerequisite for compensation growth. Denver's student-performance-based increment system and student-choice market pressure offer models. Don's perspective reveals frustration with education's industrial organization preventing teacher professionalization and corresponding compensation growth.

Undated Vocational HS (179 words):** Don addresses vocational secondary education's decline, identifying both obvious factors (facility… ▸ more
(179 words):** Don addresses vocational secondary education's decline, identifying both obvious factors (facility costs, image, program obsolescence) and deeper issues: academic-general education conflation inflating academic credit requirements. However, he notes research reveals no course-configuration superiority for further education or employment. While acknowledging high-technology economy needs educated workers, Don questions whether additional academic courses provide claimed breadth. He cites Senate report supporting core competencies: reading, writing, math, computer science, societal knowledge, learning-to-learn, teamwork, adaptability, information synthesis. Critically, Don argues these skills aren't content-driven but approach-dependent. Vocational welding courses often better develop core competencies than traditional history classrooms: real projects, responsibility assumption, authentic work habits, freedom of movement—practices schools often discourage. Don proposes secondary programs group subjects under clusters (science, social studies, communications, business, technology), allowing individual courses to serve general or vocational purposes depending on instructor approach and learner intent. He advocates eliminating academic-vocational distinctions, allowing student choice to guide educational decisions.

Undated scan0001 A brief document on occupational standards focusing on the distinction between what someone can do versus how well they… ▸ more
A brief document on occupational standards focusing on the distinction between what someone can do versus how well they do it. Drawing on his federal labor department experience, Don notes that standards discussions historically focused on training inputs rather than performance outputs. He argues existing standards emphasize range of tasks but neglect performance levels, illustrated by the difference between apprentice and journeyperson performance on the same skill. Don advocates for identifying broad performance criteria (quality, speed, supervision required) and establishing employment-based levels that are recognized by practitioners, clearly stated, readily understood, and easily applied. He contends the challenge remains unmet of creating standards that balance comprehensiveness with practical applicability.

Undated sort Don examines the credentialing system in Canada and who should award credentials. He questions whether displayed… ▸ more
Don examines the credentialing system in Canada and who should award credentials. He questions whether displayed diplomas and degrees actually indicate competence to serve, noting his experience as an employer revealed transcripts more useful than degrees. Don critiques how institutions award credentials after completing pre-set programs based on criteria that may not reflect occupational standards, limiting student choice and resulting in graduates following similar instruction regardless of individual interests. He contrasts academic credentials with more specific certificates and calls for reconsidering what credentials are needed, who should award them, and on what basis. Don emphasizes that teachers teach while schools sort learners, and this sorting function profoundly impacts future life choices. He questions current approaches to credentialing in addressing employer-valued competencies like communication, mathematics, history/geography knowledge, and work habits.

Undated 6 Qualifications One of Don's most important conceptual contributions, this document meticulously distinguishes between credentials… ▸ more
One of Don's most important conceptual contributions, this document meticulously distinguishes between credentials (diplomas and degrees signifying program completion) and qualifications (actual demonstrated competencies and ability to perform). Don argues that schools have become confused about their role, conflating the awarding of credentials with certifying qualifications. He proposes establishing an independent "Qualification Authority" (AQUA) to assess and certify actual competencies separately from educational institutions. This would reduce bias, enable lifelong learning, and allow educators to focus on teaching rather than sorting. The AQUA would operate on fee-for-service basis with advisory panels and serve all Atlantic Canada, potentially based on PEI. The document includes extensive discussion of how this confusion hampers both education and employment.

Don suggests the fund serve triple purposes: reflecting family ideals, perpetuating the company name, and honoring employees' contributions. Proposes annual awards options including scholarships in mechanical engineering/design, innovation leadership awards, or public lectures on design and marketing. The letter demonstrates Don's historical awareness and ability to connect heritage preservation with contemporary philanthropic giving.

Undated Assessment Concise assessment service proposal addressing practical employment gaps. Documents conversations with local employers… ▸ more
Concise assessment service proposal addressing practical employment gaps. Documents conversations with local employers revealing that while new hires are expected to have high school education, they frequently lack basic skills in mathematics (making change, mental arithmetic) and communications (written messages, giving directions). Notes that high school graduation requires 20 credits including 4 language (English/French), 2 math, 2 science, 2 social studies, with courses at 110 hours and 50% pass mark. Proposes establishing an independent assessment service to certify basic skill levels for individuals wanting or needing proof of competency—particularly for entry-level employment access. Specifies service characteristics: fee-based, open to individuals/companies/schools. Represents practical application of Don's assessment philosophy to immediate labor market needs.

Undated 5-1 Qualification description This document outlines a proposed independent qualification authority for PEI, representing a significant structural… ▸ more
This document outlines a proposed independent qualification authority for PEI, representing a significant structural reform in educational credentialing. The authority would analyze competencies, establish assessment criteria, create benchmarks, and report results without organizing competitions or ranking students. Don articulates substantial benefits including reduced assessment burden on schools, improved reporting consistency, enhanced credential comparison, mobility facilitation, and self-study encouragement. This proposal reflects his conviction that assessment and credentialing should function separately from teaching institutions, enabling more objective quality assurance.

Undated D 01 Gates This ambitious proposal to a major philanthropic funder (Gates Foundation implied) articulates a comprehensive vision… ▸ more
This ambitious proposal to a major philanthropic funder (Gates Foundation implied) articulates a comprehensive vision of education system transformation. Don argues that education improvement requires three foundational changes: increased public engagement through unaligned organizations (like those prevalent in health), market-based delivery systems enabling school choice and teacher innovation, and continuous progress models replacing age-based cohort systems. He critiques the current system as bureaucratic, inflexible monopoly failing to accommodate individual learner needs or employ customer service principles. The proposal argues for early childhood as distinct from education, expanded life skills focus, and structural separation of instruction from assessment/credentialing. This document represents Don's most ambitious systemic reform articulation.

Undated D 31 Reading A comprehensive three-stage strategy for establishing non-aligned public community groups focused on reading… ▸ more
A comprehensive three-stage strategy for establishing non-aligned public community groups focused on reading improvement. Stage One emphasizes public engagement through discussion papers, workshops, and media outreach. Stage Two includes establishing a federal charter society with membership, identifying reading as a separate subject in teacher training and certification, and creating qualification authorities for reading assessment. Stage Three involves creating training programs, publications, and community services like reading round tables and library tutoring. Don's proposal encompasses teacher professional development, system redesign through report cards and benchmarks, community information campaigns, and research documentation. This represents his most ambitious reading reform vision, integrating multiple intervention levels from policy to grassroots community action.

Undated D Math 3 ladder This proposal introduces the "math ladder" concept—a framework with clearly identified rungs representing known levels… ▸ more
This proposal introduces the "math ladder" concept—a framework with clearly identified rungs representing known levels of mathematical achievement. Don argues that the practice of social promotion prevents meaningful comparison of skill levels across institutions and inhibits employers' ability to evaluate applicants effectively. The solution involves creating a mathematical ladder (comparable to music or skating certification levels) with independently assessed achievement levels. The ladder would establish common standards, aid labor mobility, and reduce curriculum development workload. Why math specifically: it has identifiable structure (numeracy through infinite series) but lacks widely accepted competency analyses and level agreements.

Undated D Math 7 gap This detailed analysis documents the Mathematics Gap Initiative's scope and findings from Don's steering committee… ▸ more
This detailed analysis documents the Mathematics Gap Initiative's scope and findings from Don's steering committee (including Duffy, Miller, White-Hassler, Greyborn, Francis). The committee established that the math gap is real and has severe economic consequences: reduced quality of life, lower productivity, higher unemployment, lower product quality, reduced wages, and poor customer service. Root causes identified include differing perceptions of institutional roles, silo mentalities, problematic curriculum development models, and education politicization. The proposal establishes an independent assessment service certifying proficiency in everyday mathematics skills. Services would be fee-based, age-neutral, providing certificates (not credentials) with results shared upon applicant request.

Undated 79 Not a teaching function Don distinguishes between assessment (gathering information for improvement in dialogue with learners) and grading… ▸ more
Don distinguishes between assessment (gathering information for improvement in dialogue with learners) and grading (applying standardized measurement to determine achievement levels). He argues grading is inherently a regulatory function and may damage teacher-student relationships if teachers perform it. Notes grading's high-stakes nature demands fairness and objectivity, which is why many developed countries use independent qualification authorities. Proposes an Atlantic Qualification Authority to serve the region, potentially hosted in PEI. Reports significant reader response to his earlier "Grades as Capitalism" article with mixed support and varying viewpoints. Discusses student absenteeism, citing Washington Post research showing 40% of high-absence students from low-income families benefit from sense of belonging, academic purpose, relevant instruction, career pathways, homework support, and strong relationships.

Don welcomes the education system upheaval occurring across Canada. Ontario plans to introduce math teaching assessments for new teachers; Alberta undertaking curriculum overhaul with focus on essential skills and past-practice review. His optimism about educational "turmoil" reflects belief that these disruptions enable meaningful systemic improvement. Demonstrates Don's long view of education transformation.

Undated 86 Peformance factors Don outlines shift from student grades to learner performance appraisal. Performance on a subject depends on four… ▸ more
Don outlines shift from student grades to learner performance appraisal. Performance on a subject depends on four factors: number of competencies mastered, range demonstrated, clustering nature/extent, and performance levels on specific competencies. Individual skill performance differs based on: consistency across skills, assistance needed, completion time, math fluency, discussion comfort, complex problem-solving, and result accuracy. Dynamic rating scales include significant judgment components that some record systems cannot accommodate. References Harvard study of 6,000 college freshmen showing algebra, geometry, and trigonometry (not calculus) predict college success. Chicago schools developing soft skills produced students with higher grades, fewer absences, better behavior, improved graduation rates, and increased college attendance. London Times reports secondary math often as irrelevant as ancient Greek; National Numeracy head proposes dividing math GCSE into classical (optional) and modern tracks.

Undated 95 School transcripts Don advocates for more comprehensive student transcripts that detail specific competencies achieved rather than just… ▸ more
Don advocates for more comprehensive student transcripts that detail specific competencies achieved rather than just overall grades. Using Math 421A as an example, he proposes breaking down transcripts into component skills (sequences, polynomials, geometry, trigonometry, data management) and rating progress on individual outcomes. He suggests personal profiles and common rating scales across all grade levels and subjects would improve communication throughout the education system. This reflects his broader commitment to outcome-based education and transparency in student achievement reporting.

Undated 96 j Courses Don explores the historical J-Course system (Job Instruction Training, Job Relations Training, Job Methods Training)… ▸ more
Don explores the historical J-Course system (Job Instruction Training, Job Relations Training, Job Methods Training) from WWII Canada, designed to rapidly upskill workers entering manufacturing. These focused, brief courses emphasized practical job skills with reference cards for worker review. Don advocates reviving this model for modern workforce development, seeing parallels with contemporary mini-credentials and apprenticeship programs. He draws on personal experience teaching instructors at community colleges and trade institutes, emphasizing the value of focused, skills-based education for employment and lifelong learning.

Undated 04 Learning path Interim report describing development of a learning path model based on consultation with young people about helpful… ▸ more
Interim report describing development of a learning path model based on consultation with young people about helpful learning supports. Identifies five learning path components: entry point, exploration (information gathering, observation, assessment, counselling), engagement in teaching-learning process (instruction, self-study, practice, feedback), documentation (credentials/qualifications), and exit point. Demonstrates that learners cycle through process differently based on individual style and pace, supporting spiral or learner-centered curriculum approach. Emphasizes that learning is broad concept encompassing all experience, not just schooling. Notes learning path remains work in progress open to continued development and input.

This deeply personal essay reveals Don's own educational formation through apprenticeship-style learning on his family's mixed farm along the Miramichi in New Brunswick. Letter from Don distinguishing between mathematics (theoretical study of number, quantity, and space) and numeracy (applied confidence in using numbers for practical life purposes). He argues that citizenship education would benefit from emphasis on numeracy rather than pure mathematics, drawing on definitions and frameworks from Learning Wales, Ontario, and New Zealand education ministries. He suggests a curriculum shift from math to numeracy could significantly improve mathematical literacy levels in PEI.

Undated 1- CMEC -Atlantic This letter (undated) addresses the Council of Ministers of Education, Canada (CMEC) regarding school-to-work… ▸ more
This letter (undated) addresses the Council of Ministers of Education, Canada (CMEC) regarding school-to-work transitions, disagreeing that the problem is primarily student-focused; Don attributes it to school system rigidity. He recommends surveying employers about needed competencies, consulting educators during curriculum outcomes identification, examining various curriculum development options, and switching assessment from traditional grades to externally-referenced criteria (matching school and real-life standards), as advocated by outcomes expert William Spadey. He urges CMEC to disconnect performance levels from grade levels and disconnect assessment from credentials in favor of qualifications, referencing qualification frameworks used in most developed countries.

Undated D 25 Skill DevelopmentNews A letter critiquing educators' neglect of "skill development" in favor of knowledge focus. Don observes that teacher… ▸ more
A letter critiquing educators' neglect of "skill development" in favor of knowledge focus. Don observes that teacher compensation and student grades emphasize knowledge acquisition (lowest level of Bloom's taxonomy) rather than ability to apply knowledge. He defines skill development as "an educational process of increasing a person's ability to do something" and argues it applies across all programs and subjects. The letter advocates for higher achievement standards and greater precision in educational terminology to increase accountability.

Undated 04 Barriers to educational reform Don identifies three overlooked barriers to educational reform: employer recruitment practices that contradict their… ▸ more
Don identifies three overlooked barriers to educational reform: employer recruitment practices that contradict their stated need for employability skills, professional certification requirements locked into traditional course offerings, and rigid college/university entrance requirements. He argues employers should lead by basing hiring on competencies rather than credentials; professionals should base designations on performance not time served; and post-secondary institutions should relax course rigidity. He ends optimistically: with ministerial directives, reform could advance through hiring and promotion based on competencies rather than credentials.

Undated 08 Critical issues Don recounts a contracted report on critical secondary education issues, prepared with a former university president,… ▸ more
Don recounts a contracted report on critical secondary education issues, prepared with a former university president, school superintendent, and deputy minister. The report identified six critical issues and proposed fourteen demonstration projects addressing application of information technology, teacher preparation, accountability, school leadership, poorly served students, and linkage/coordination. Proposed pilots included alternative school models, competency-based programs, student part-time employment guidelines, and an open system allowing exam challenges without coursework. Don notes many suggestions remain valid for demonstration projects and references the Canadian Restructured School Project (CRSP) arising from this work.

Undated 11 Learning About Learning Don reflects on his teaching journey, commencing at age 17 at Teacher's College Fredericton, then teaching rural… ▸ more
Don reflects on his teaching journey, commencing at age 17 at Teacher's College Fredericton, then teaching rural one-room schools and secondary/post-secondary levels. He laments insufficient focus on the teaching-learning process during his training and advocates every school should publish a learning model focused on learner activities (not teacher activities). He provides a practical exercise: sketch steps from course entry to exit, sequence them, identify optional paths, and ask how you know the sequence is correct. Don emphasizes that developing a learning model takes minutes initially but lifetimes to perfect—and wishes he'd developed one before his teaching career.

Undated 12 Outcome framework vital to succeeding Don discusses recent outcomes-based education conversations with national Sectoral Councils and colleagues. He reports… ▸ more
Don discusses recent outcomes-based education conversations with national Sectoral Councils and colleagues. He reports good news: outcomes are universally accepted as the educational design starting point. However, he identifies two critical gaps: need for common outcome terminology and need for an outcomes structure/framework. Using a farm shed-building analogy, Don explains that having clear architectural design before selecting lumber (outcomes) accelerates progress. He connects DACUM (occupational analysis) to broader "life roles" (learner, producer, citizen, family member, consumer), proposing a outcomes hierarchy: life roles, occupation, general competencies, specific competencies, elaborations. He emphasizes that outcome architecture knowledge pays "great dividends."

Undated Clarity 2 ocx Don explores the fundamental distinction between "education" and "going to school," arguing they are often conflated.… ▸ more
Don explores the fundamental distinction between "education" and "going to school," arguing they are often conflated. He notes that the World Bank warns against treating these as synonymous, yet the practice persists broadly. Don defines education as encompassing life experiences in home, school, and life itself, and argues a good education prepares people for everyday life decisions. He criticizes the absence of definitions in provincial education literature and compares nonprofits in health (more focused on health issues) versus education, suggesting education lacks definitional clarity present in other sectors.

A brief comparative framework contrasting decision-making authority in health versus education. In health, doctors make individual treatment decisions while referencing national standards; in education, teachers apply department-approved actions without national standards. The contrast illustrates Don's concern that education lacks the professional autonomy and evidence-based standards he considers necessary for effective service delivery.

A comparative analysis of departmental roles across Canadian provinces, compiled by Don. Shows PEI defines its role narrowly as delivering "high quality programs and curriculum" while other provinces articulate broader missions: New Brunswick (administration of public education system), Nova Scotia (children's education including early years), Newfoundland & Labrador (leadership on child development, care, K-12, libraries, postsecondary), Ontario (policy, funding, curriculum across all levels), Manitoba (equity and social justice), Alberta (enabling effective functioning in work and life), and British Columbia (enabling individual potential and healthy society). Don's compilation demonstrates that PEI's narrow definition is outlier.

Undated Instruction model (155 words):** Don Glendenning presents a competency-based instructional model emphasizing practical skill development… ▸ more
(155 words):** Don Glendenning presents a competency-based instructional model emphasizing practical skill development aligned with field practice. The model includes identifying work-relevant competencies, establishing performance standards, creating advisory committees from served communities, using dynamic rating scales, competency-by-competency progress reporting, learner profiles, self-evaluation, and community engagement. Key features include basing programs and assessments on demonstrated performance rather than time served, providing individualized tutoring as needed, and diagnosing learner progress through discussion and learning plans. This model reflects Don's belief that education should focus on demonstrated competency rather than seat time, addressing individual learner needs, and maintaining strong community connections—themes recurring throughout his reform proposals.

Undated MISSION 3 An expanded exploration of education system missions globally, including statements from Maine, Bangor, Alberta, and… ▸ more
An expanded exploration of education system missions globally, including statements from Maine, Bangor, Alberta, and other jurisdictions. Glendenning documents that PEI's education mission "captures neither the spirit, breadth, significance nor urgency of the education task." He identifies systemic problems: the department operates in "public instruction" mode rather than educational development, lacks engagement with 21st-century competencies, ignores soft skills, and shows no interest in broader Islander education. His conclusion: PEI's government is unclear about what it aims to accomplish through education. He proposes a new mission: "Help Islanders develop knowledge, skills and other attributes needed for life in 21st century" and recommends mission statement development as a valuable staff development activity.

Undated Trends Essay on conceptual clarity as precondition for educational effectiveness. Glendenning argues that lack of clarity may… ▸ more
Essay on conceptual clarity as precondition for educational effectiveness. Glendenning argues that lack of clarity may arise from the idea itself, the person promoting it, or the recipient's understanding. He emphasizes that conceptual clarity is prerequisite for analysis and that term restriction may be necessary to prevent dilution and meaninglessness. Lack of clarity signals potential conflicting ideas or internal disagreement. Lists 12 specific comments/actions: prepare taxonomy, establish clear purpose, organize by functions, review accountabilities, check communication, monitor engagement, strike advisory bodies, establish individual profiles, distinguish education from schooling, keep community informed.

Undated Framework-2 for change 2 Education framework summary - (major work). Read · Download
Undated Levels 2 This document focuses specifically on achievement levels in literacy and numeracy, chosen because they are foundational… ▸ more
This document focuses specifically on achievement levels in literacy and numeracy, chosen because they are foundational to modern society, unique in their educational roles, and critical budget priorities. Don cites European Commission research showing that increasing national average educational attainment by one year increases productivity by 6.2% immediately and 3.1% long-term. The paper outlines core beliefs: people differ in conditions of birth, prior learning, and aspirations; achievement levels involve range and level of demonstrated skills; sorting is not a school function; outcomes derive from subject examination rather than learner characteristics; and levels are non-competitive status indicators. Current issues include teacher-sorting conflicts, poorly measurable outcomes, absent benchmarks, complicated assessment systems, grade-level confusion, social promotion, and inadequate report cards.

Undated Post secondary Low cost degrees Tamar Lewin reports on Texas and Florida initiatives to create $10,000 bachelor's degrees as a response to escalating… ▸ more
Tamar Lewin reports on Texas and Florida initiatives to create $10,000 bachelor's degrees as a response to escalating costs and student debt. Governor Rick Perry and Rick Scott launched programs to challenge universities to develop efficient degree programs using online learning and new pedagogies. Early results show limited uptake: Broward College offered low-cost degrees in only four specialized programs with 80-student target, while Miami Dade drew 62 students. Article notes most programs involve "re-pricing" rather than fundamental redesign, and federal/state aid means most students already pay under $10,000.

Undated Issues-2 Education (160 words):** Don Glendenning defines systemic education issues and proposes functional solutions. He reiterates that… ▸ more
(160 words):** Don Glendenning defines systemic education issues and proposes functional solutions. He reiterates that education—preparation for life roles—differs fundamentally from schooling and requires comprehensive service delivery (policy, governance, instruction, resources, information, counseling, quality assurance). Key issues include: inadequate public statements of educational purpose and principles; limited Department mandate focused narrowly on curriculum delivery rather than broad educational leadership; absence of comprehensive vision statements; underserved information functions; conflicting quality assurance approaches; and disconnection from communities served. Don advocates for independent certification bodies (as in apprenticeships) to separate teaching from grading, allowing teachers to focus on learning without compromising their role. He emphasizes that community engagement and clear functional organization are essential for system improvement. The document calls for collaborative conversations between educators and communities to develop shared understanding.

Undated Instruction (130 words):** This document outlines teaching competencies and instructional steps necessary for educator development.… ▸ more
(130 words):** This document outlines teaching competencies and instructional steps necessary for educator development. Don identifies nine instructional competency areas: developing personal and professional competencies, instructional techniques, learning materials, learning environments, assisting self-directed and occupational learning, evaluating learner progress, administrative functions, and community interaction. The document compares Alberta's teaching competencies (communication, organization, classroom management, facilitation, assessment, collaboration, caring, flexibility) with broader instructional frameworks. The emphasis on community interaction and broad competency development suggests Don's view that teaching extends beyond classroom delivery to include community engagement and systemic responsibility.

Undated Issues-8 Education (125 words):** This condensed summary presents eight key issues Don Glendenning identifies in the education system: (1)… ▸ more
(125 words):** This condensed summary presents eight key issues Don Glendenning identifies in the education system: (1) education and schooling used interchangeably; (2) absent taxonomy for educational concepts; (3) education functions/services unidentified; (4) system relies on schools rather than comprehensive education system; (5) insufficient school autonomy despite OECD evidence supporting it; (6) unclear accountabilities; (7) limited services for independent learners; (8) assessment dependent on written tests; inadequate achievement reporting; system lacks benchmarks. This brief outline captures Don's core concerns in structured format, suggesting preparation for policy discussions or public communication. The emphasis on missing frameworks, inadequate reporting, and system-wide communication gaps aligns with his central argument that educational clarity and purpose are foundational.

Undated Math projects (115 words):** Don Glendenning outlines potential mathematics education projects designed to align curriculum with… ▸ more
(115 words):** Don Glendenning outlines potential mathematics education projects designed to align curriculum with practical application and competency-based assessment. Projects include: correlating PISA test content with actual community mathematics use; analyzing course content relevance to community application; developing performance-based rating scales; identifying competencies across grade levels; providing competency lists to students; adopting New Brunswick's Grade 5 citizenship mathematics standards; questioning ranking systems; implementing self-rating practices; developing learning platforms; and establishing comprehensive learning competency frameworks. These projects reflect Don's commitment to making mathematics education relevant, practical, and competency-focused rather than grade-focused. They align with his systemic reform vision of education serving practical life and work needs.

Undated Personal Investment 1 Glendenning identifies systemic barriers to reform stemming from limited personal investment by education leaders. He… ▸ more
Glendenning identifies systemic barriers to reform stemming from limited personal investment by education leaders. He catalogs problems: compensation based on university credentials rather than performance; dependence on outside experts; inadequate information in reporting systems; selective PISA result citation without implementing recommendations; reluctance to speak publicly about education; lack of conceptual clarity; frequent administrative turnover with government changes; political appointment of leadership; minimal educational research; and emphasis on delivery rather than development. The document suggests these structural incentives undermine leadership commitment to systemic reform and student learning outcomes. His underlying argument: systemic problems are not primarily pedagogical but rather structural, political, and cultural.

Undated Sorting-credentialing An expanded version of the credentialing distinction, providing detailed definitions of credentials as documents issued… ▸ more
An expanded version of the credentialing distinction, providing detailed definitions of credentials as documents issued by officially designated bodies following assessment, indicating capability to perform specified actions. Certification is reframed as a sorting process separate from education, carried out by independent bodies, attesting to ability to perform services, potentially subject to renewal. Don emphasizes certification is not itself part of any learning model. This refined articulation demonstrates his conviction that fundamental system reform requires clarity about institutional roles and separation of educational delivery from credentialing and certification functions.

Undated NZQA Don's notes on New Zealand's National Qualifications Framework development, examining why NZQA emerged: gaps in… ▸ more
Don's notes on New Zealand's National Qualifications Framework development, examining why NZQA emerged: gaps in recognized qualifications, unclear linkages between qualifications, unclear training access, non-recognition of prior learning, inconsistent terminology, and international mobility needs. Key principles include simplification, open learning pathways, lifelong learning emphasis, and performance-based assessment. The framework contains eight levels covering age six upward, using Units of Learning that may count toward multiple qualifications. Units include title, credit rating, performance criteria, and accreditation processes. Schools are accredited emphasizing teaching quality management, monitored by national staff. Learners receive records showing achieved units and earned certificates/diplomas.
